<div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zinc_spectrum_visible.png" title="Color lines in a spectral range"><img alt="Color lines in a spectral range" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="7430" height="41" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Zinc_spectrum_visible.png/240px-Zinc_spectrum_visible.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Zinc_spectrum_visible.png/360px-Zinc_spectrum_visible.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Zinc_spectrum_visible.png/480px-Zinc_spectrum_visible.png 2x" width="240"/></a></div></div><strong><a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">Spectral lines</a> of zinc</strong></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#cccccc">Other properties</th></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">Crystal structure</a></th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_zinc" title="Template:Infobox zinc"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_zinc" title="Template talk:Infobox zinc"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_zinc&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Zinc</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Zn</b> and atomic number 30. It is the first element in <a href="/wiki/Group_12_element" title="Group 12 element">group 12</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>. In some respects zinc is chemically similar to <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>: both elements exhibit only one normal oxidation state (+2), and the Zn<sup>2+</sup> and Mg<sup>2+</sup> <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> are of similar size. Zinc is the 24th most abundant <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">element in Earth's crust</a> and has five stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>. The most common zinc <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> is <a href="/wiki/Sphalerite" title="Sphalerite">sphalerite</a> (zinc blende), a <a href="/wiki/Zinc_sulfide" title="Zinc sulfide">zinc sulfide</a> mineral. The largest workable lodes are in Australia, Asia, and the United States. Zinc is refined by <a href="/wiki/Froth_flotation" title="Froth flotation">froth flotation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roasting_(metallurgy)" title="Roasting (metallurgy)">roasting</a>, and final <a href="/wiki/Extractive_metallurgy" title="Extractive metallurgy">extraction</a> using <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> (<a href="/wiki/Electrowinning" title="Electrowinning">electrowinning</a>).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">Brass</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> and zinc in various proportions, was used as early as the third millennium BC in the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalmykia" title="Kalmykia">Kalmykia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, and the second millennium BC in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_India" title="West India">West India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, Iraq, and <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p1201_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p1201-4">[4]</a></sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jas5_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-jas5-5">[5]</a></sup> Zinc <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> was not produced on a large scale until the 12th century in India, though it was known to the ancient Romans and Greeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The mines of <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a> have given definite evidence of zinc production going back to the 6th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> To date, the oldest evidence of pure zinc comes from Zawar, in Rajasthan, as early as the 9th century AD when a distillation process was employed to make pure zinc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">Alchemists</a> burned zinc in air to form what they called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosopher%27s_wool" title="Philosopher's wool">philosopher's wool</a>" or "white snow".
</p><p>The element was probably named by the alchemist <a href="/wiki/Paracelsus" title="Paracelsus">Paracelsus</a> after the German word <i>Zinke</i> (prong, tooth). German chemist <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Sigismund_Marggraf" title="Andreas Sigismund Marggraf">Andreas Sigismund Marggraf</a> is credited with discovering pure metallic zinc in 1746. Work by <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Galvani" title="Luigi Galvani">Luigi Galvani</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">Alessandro Volta</a> uncovered the electrochemical properties of zinc by 1800. <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">Corrosion</a>-resistant <a href="/wiki/Galvanization" title="Galvanization">zinc plating</a> of iron (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot-dip_galvanizing" title="Hot-dip galvanizing">hot-dip galvanizing</a>) is the major application for zinc. Other applications are in electrical <a href="/wiki/Zinc%E2%80%93carbon_battery" title="Zinc–carbon battery">batteries</a>, small non-structural castings, and alloys such as <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brass</a>. A variety of zinc compounds are commonly used, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_carbonate" title="Zinc carbonate">zinc carbonate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zinc_gluconate" title="Zinc gluconate">zinc gluconate</a> (as dietary supplements), <a href="/wiki/Zinc_chloride" title="Zinc chloride">zinc chloride</a> (in deodorants), <a href="/wiki/Zinc_pyrithione" title="Zinc pyrithione">zinc pyrithione</a> (anti-<a href="/wiki/Dandruff" title="Dandruff">dandruff</a> shampoos), <a href="/wiki/Zinc_sulfide" title="Zinc sulfide">zinc sulfide</a> (in luminescent paints), and zinc methyl or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_diethyl" title="Zinc diethyl">zinc diethyl</a> in the organic laboratory.
</p><p>Zinc is an <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">essential mineral</a>, including to prenatal and postnatal development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hambridge2007_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hambridge2007-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zinc_deficiency" title="Zinc deficiency">Zinc deficiency</a> affects about two billion people in the developing world and is associated with many diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prasad2003_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prasad2003-10">[10]</a></sup> In children, deficiency causes growth retardation, delayed sexual maturation, infection susceptibility, and <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hambridge2007_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hambridge2007-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">Enzymes</a> with a zinc atom in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prosthetic_groups" title="Prosthetic groups">reactive center</a> are widespread in biochemistry, such as <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_dehydrogenase" title="Alcohol dehydrogenase">alcohol dehydrogenase</a> in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Consumption of excess zinc can cause <a href="/wiki/Ataxia" title="Ataxia">ataxia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lethargy" title="Lethargy">lethargy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Copper_deficiency" title="Copper deficiency">copper deficiency</a>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Precautions"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Precautions</span></a>
