<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Zinc is a bluish-white, lustrous, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diamagnetic" title="Diamagnetic">diamagnetic</a> metal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCp4-41_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRCp4-41-12">[12]</a></sup> though most common commercial grades of the metal have a dull finish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heiserman1992p123_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heiserman1992p123-13">[13]</a></sup> It is somewhat less dense than <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and has a hexagonal <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal structure</a>, with a distorted form of <a href="/wiki/Close-packing_of_equal_spheres" title="Close-packing of equal spheres">hexagonal close packing</a>, in which each atom has six nearest neighbors (at 265.9 pm) in its own plane and six others at a greater distance of 290.6 pm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The metal is hard and brittle at most temperatures but becomes malleable between 100 and 150 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCp4-41_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-CRCp4-41-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heiserman1992p123_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Heiserman1992p123-13">[13]</a></sup> Above 210 °C, the metal becomes brittle again and can be pulverized by beating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Zinc is a fair <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">conductor of electricity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCp4-41_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-CRCp4-41-12">[12]</a></sup> For a metal, zinc has relatively low melting (419.5 °C) and boiling points (907 °C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZincMetalProps_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZincMetalProps-16">[16]</a></sup> The melting point is the lowest of all the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D-block" title="D-block">d-block</a> metals aside from <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a>; for this, among other reasons, zinc, cadmium, and mercury are often not considered to be <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metals</a> like the rest of the d-block metals are.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZincMetalProps_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-ZincMetalProps-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> contain zinc, including brass. Other metals long known to form binary alloys with zinc are <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">tellurium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Although neither zinc nor <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a> are <a href="/wiki/Ferromagnetism" title="Ferromagnetism">ferromagnetic</a>, their alloy <span class="chemf nowrap">ZrZn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> exhibits ferromagnetism below 35 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCp4-41_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-CRCp4-41-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>A bar of zinc generates a characteristic sound when bent, similar to <a href="/wiki/Tin_cry" title="Tin cry">tin cry</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Zinc_minerals" title="Category:Zinc minerals">Zinc minerals</a></div>
<p>Zinc makes up about 75 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> (0.0075%) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a>, making it the 24th most abundant element. Soil contains zinc in 5–770 ppm with an average 64 ppm. <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">Seawater</a> has only 30 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_billion" title="Parts per billion">ppb</a> and the atmosphere, 0.1–4 µg/m<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p503_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p503-18">[18]</a></sup> The element is normally found in association with other <a href="/wiki/Base_metal" title="Base metal">base metals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehto1968p822_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lehto1968p822-19">[19]</a></sup> Zinc is a <a href="/wiki/Goldschmidt_classification#Chalcophile_elements" title="Goldschmidt classification">chalcophile</a>, meaning the element is more likely to be found in minerals together with <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> and other heavy <a href="/wiki/Chalcogen" title="Chalcogen">chalcogens</a>, rather than with the light chalcogen <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> or with non-chalcogen electronegative elements such as the <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">Sulfides</a> formed as the crust solidified under the <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reducing</a> conditions of the early Earth's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p1202_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p1202-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sphalerite" title="Sphalerite">Sphalerite</a>, which is a form of zinc sulfide, is the most heavily mined zinc-containing ore because its concentrate contains 60–62% zinc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehto1968p822_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lehto1968p822-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other source minerals for zinc include <a href="/wiki/Smithsonite" title="Smithsonite">smithsonite</a> (zinc <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a>), <a href="/wiki/Hemimorphite" title="Hemimorphite">hemimorphite</a> (zinc <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a>), <a href="/wiki/Wurtzite" title="Wurtzite">wurtzite</a> (another zinc sulfide), and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Hydrozincite" title="Hydrozincite">hydrozincite</a> (basic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_carbonate" title="Zinc carbonate">zinc carbonate</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p502_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p502-21">[21]</a></sup> With the exception of wurtzite, all these other minerals were formed by weathering of the primordial zinc sulfides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p1202_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p1202-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Identified world zinc resources total about 1.9–2.8 billion <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSMCS2015_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGSMCS2015-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Large deposits are in Australia, Canada and the United States, with the largest reserves in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p1202_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p1202-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The most recent estimate of reserve base for zinc (meets specified minimum physical criteria related to current mining and production practices) was made in 2009 and calculated to be roughly 480 Mt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSMCS2009_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGSMCS2009-26">[26]</a></sup> Zinc reserves, on the other hand, are geologically identified ore bodies whose suitability for recovery is economically based (location, grade, quality, and quantity) at the time of determination. Since exploration and mine development is an ongoing process, the amount of zinc reserves is not a fixed number and sustainability of zinc ore supplies cannot be judged by simply extrapolating the combined mine life of today's zinc mines. This concept is well supported by data from the United States Geological Survey (USGS), which illustrates that although refined zinc production increased 80% between 1990 and 2010, the reserve lifetime for zinc has remained unchanged. About 346 million tonnes have been extracted throughout history to 2002, and scholars have estimated that about 109–305 million tonnes are in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sphalerite4.jpg"><img alt="A black shiny lump of solid with uneven surface" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="343" data-file-width="372" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Sphalerite4.jpg/220px-Sphalerite4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Sphalerite4.jpg/330px-Sphalerite4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2f/Sphalerite4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sphalerite4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sphalerite" title="Sphalerite">Sphalerite</a> (ZnS)</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_zinc" title="Isotopes of zinc">Isotopes of zinc</a></div>
<p>Five <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> of zinc occur in nature. <sup>64</sup>Zn is the most abundant isotope (48.63% <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">natural abundance</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-30">[30]</a></sup> That isotope has such a long <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a>, at <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7026135697680000000♠</span>4.3<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>18</sup> years</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> that its radioactivity can be ignored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-32">[32]</a></sup> Similarly, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">70</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span> (0.6%), with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7023410248800000000♠</span>1.3<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>16</sup> years</span> is not usually considered to be radioactive. The other isotopes found in nature are <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">66</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span> (28%), <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">67</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span> (4%) and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">68</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span> (19%).
</p><p>Several dozen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotope" title="Radioisotope">radioisotopes</a> have been characterized. <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">65</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span>, which has a half-life of 243.66 days, is the least active radioisotope, followed by <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">72</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span> with a half-life of 46.5 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-30">[30]</a></sup> Zinc has 10 <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomers</a>. <sup>69m</sup>Zn has the longest half-life, 13.76 h.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-30">[30]</a></sup> The superscript <i>m</i> indicates a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metastable" title="Metastable">metastable</a> isotope. The nucleus of a metastable isotope is in an <a href="/wiki/Excited_state" title="Excited state">excited state</a> and will return to the <a href="/wiki/Ground_state" title="Ground state">ground state</a> by emitting a <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a> in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a>. <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">61</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span> has three excited metastable states and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">73</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span> has two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The isotopes <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">65</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">71</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">77</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span> and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">78</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span> each have only one excited metastable state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decay_mode" title="Decay mode">decay mode</a> of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotope" title="Radioisotope">radioisotope</a> of zinc with a <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass number</a> lower than 66 is <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">decay product</a> resulting from electron capture is an isotope of copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">n</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">30</sub></span>Zn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a> → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">n</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">29</sub></span>Cu<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The most common decay mode of a radioisotope of zinc with mass number higher than 66 is <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> (β<sup>−</sup>), which produces an isotope of <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_30-5"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">n</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">30</sub></span>Zn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">n</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">31</sub></span>Ga<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a> + <a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino#Electron_antineutrino" title="Electron neutrino"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">ν</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span></a></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds_and_chemistry">Compounds and chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Compounds and chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Compounds_of_zinc" title="Compounds of zinc">Compounds of zinc</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactivity">Reactivity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Reactivity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Clemmensen_reduction" title="Clemmensen reduction">Clemmensen reduction</a></div>
<p>Zinc has an <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">electron configuration</a> of [Ar]3d<sup>10</sup>4s<sup>2</sup> and is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Group_12_element" title="Group 12 element">group 12</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>. It is a moderately reactive metal and strong <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2006p8-29_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2006p8-29-34">[34]</a></sup> The surface of the pure metal <a href="/wiki/Tarnish" title="Tarnish">tarnishes</a> quickly, eventually forming a protective <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivating</a> layer of the basic <a href="/wiki/Hydrozincite" title="Hydrozincite">zinc carbonate</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">Zn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>(CO<sub>3</sub>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, by reaction with atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> This layer helps prevent further reaction with air and water.
</p><p>Zinc burns in air with a bright bluish-green flame, giving off fumes of <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide" title="Zinc oxide">zinc oxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-36">[36]</a></sup> Zinc reacts readily with <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkalis</a> and other non-metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Extremely pure zinc reacts only slowly at <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a> with acids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-36">[36]</a></sup> Strong acids, such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, can remove the passivating layer and subsequent reaction with water releases hydrogen gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The chemistry of zinc is dominated by the +2 oxidation state. When compounds in this oxidation state are formed, the outer <a href="/wiki/Electron_shell" title="Electron shell">shell</a> <i>s</i> electrons are lost, yielding a bare zinc ion with the electronic configuration [Ar]3d<sup>10</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In aqueous solution an octahedral complex, <span class="chemf nowrap">[Zn(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<sub>6</sub>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> is the predominant species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volatilization" title="Volatilization">volatilization</a> of zinc in combination with zinc chloride at temperatures above 285 °C indicates the formation of <span class="chemf nowrap">Zn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, a zinc compound with a +1 oxidation state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-36">[36]</a></sup> No compounds of zinc in oxidation states other than +1 or +2 are known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Calculations indicate that a zinc compound with the oxidation state of +4 is unlikely to exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zinc chemistry is similar to the chemistry of the late first-row transition metals, <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> and copper, though it has a filled d-shell and compounds are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diamagnetic" title="Diamagnetic">diamagnetic</a> and mostly colorless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p1206_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p1206-42">[42]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionic_radii" title="Ionic radii">ionic radii</a> of zinc and magnesium happen to be nearly identical. Because of this some of the equivalent salts have the same <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal structure</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> and in other circumstances where ionic radius is a determining factor, the chemistry of zinc has much in common with that of magnesium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-36">[36]</a></sup> In other respects, there is little similarity with the late first-row transition metals. Zinc tends to form bonds with a greater degree of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covalency" title="Covalency">covalency</a> and much more stable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_(chemistry)" title="Complex (chemistry)">complexes</a> with <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">N</a>- and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">S</a>- donors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p1206_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p1206-42">[42]</a></sup> Complexes of zinc are mostly 4- or 6- <a href="/wiki/Coordinate_covalent_bond" title="Coordinate covalent bond">coordinate</a> although 5-coordinate complexes are known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl_36-5"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Zinc.28I.29_compounds"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Zinc(I)_compounds">Zinc(I) compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Zinc(I) compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Zinc(I) compounds are rare and need bulky ligands to stabilize the low oxidation state. Most zinc(I) compounds contain formally the [Zn<sub>2</sub>]<sup>2+</sup> core, which is analogous to the [Hg<sub>2</sub>]<sup>2+</sup> dimeric cation present in <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>(I) compounds. The <a href="/wiki/Diamagnetism" title="Diamagnetism">diamagnetic</a> nature of the ion confirms its dimeric structure. The first zinc(I) compound containing the Zn–Zn bond, <a href="/wiki/Decamethyldizincocene" title="Decamethyldizincocene">(η<sup>5</sup>-C<sub>5</sub>Me<sub>5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>Zn<sub>2</sub></a>, is also the first <a href="/wiki/Metallocene" title="Metallocene">dimetallocene</a>. The [Zn<sub>2</sub>]<sup>2+</sup> ion rapidly <a href="/wiki/Disproportionation" title="Disproportionation">disproportionates</a> into zinc metal and zinc(II), and has been obtained only a yellow glass only by cooling a solution of metallic zinc in molten ZnCl<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Zinc.28II.29_compounds"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Zinc(II)_compounds">Zinc(II) compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Zinc(II) compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zinc_acetate.JPG"><img alt="Sheets of zinc acetate formed by slow evaporation" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="527" data-file-width="922" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Zinc_acetate.JPG/170px-Zinc_acetate.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Zinc_acetate.JPG/255px-Zinc_acetate.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Zinc_acetate.JPG/340px-Zinc_acetate.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zinc_acetate.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Zinc acetate</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zinc_chloride.jpg"><img alt="White lumped powder on a glass plate" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="663" data-file-width="759" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Zinc_chloride.jpg/170px-Zinc_chloride.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Zinc_chloride.jpg/255px-Zinc_chloride.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Zinc_chloride.jpg/340px-Zinc_chloride.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zinc_chloride.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Zinc chloride</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_compound" title="Binary compound">Binary compounds</a> of zinc are known for most of the <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloids</a> and all the <a href="/wiki/Nonmetal" title="Nonmetal">nonmetals</a> except the <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gases</a>. The oxide <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide" title="Zinc oxide">ZnO</a> is a white powder that is nearly insoluble in neutral aqueous solutions, but is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphoteric" title="Amphoteric">amphoteric</a>, dissolving in both strong basic and acidic solutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl_36-6"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-36">[36]</a></sup> The other <a href="/wiki/Chalcogen" title="Chalcogen">chalcogenides</a> (<a href="/wiki/Zinc_sulfide" title="Zinc sulfide">ZnS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc_selenide" title="Zinc selenide">ZnSe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zinc_telluride" title="Zinc telluride">ZnTe</a>) have varied applications in electronics and optics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pnictogenide" title="Pnictogenide">Pnictogenides</a> (<a href="/wiki/Zinc_nitride" title="Zinc nitride"><span class="chemf nowrap">Zn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc_phosphide" title="Zinc phosphide"><span class="chemf nowrap">Zn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>P<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc_arsenide" title="Zinc arsenide"><span class="chemf nowrap">Zn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>As<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a> and <a href="/wiki/Zinc_antimonide" title="Zinc antimonide"><span class="chemf nowrap">Zn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>Sb<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> the peroxide (<a href="/wiki/Zinc_peroxide" title="Zinc peroxide"><span class="chemf nowrap">ZnO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>), the hydride (<a href="/wiki/Zinc_hydride" title="Zinc hydride"><span class="chemf nowrap">ZnH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>), and the carbide (<span class="chemf nowrap">ZnC<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) are also known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Of the four <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc_fluoride" title="Zinc fluoride"><span class="chemf nowrap">ZnF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a> has the most ionic character, while the others (<a href="/wiki/Zinc_chloride" title="Zinc chloride"><span class="chemf nowrap">ZnCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc_bromide" title="Zinc bromide"><span class="chemf nowrap">ZnBr<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zinc_iodide" title="Zinc iodide"><span class="chemf nowrap">ZnI<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>) have relatively low melting points and are considered to have more covalent character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p1211_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p1211-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>In weak basic solutions containing <span class="chemf nowrap">Zn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ions, the hydroxide <a href="/wiki/Zinc_hydroxide" title="Zinc hydroxide"><span class="chemf nowrap">Zn(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a> forms as a white <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitate" title="Precipitate">precipitate</a>. In stronger alkaline solutions, this hydroxide is dissolved to form zincates (<a href="/wiki/Zincate" title="Zincate"><span class="chemf nowrap">[Zn(OH)<sub>4</sub>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl_36-7"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-36">[36]</a></sup> The nitrate <a href="/wiki/Zinc_nitrate" title="Zinc nitrate"><span class="chemf nowrap">Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>, chlorate <a href="/wiki/Zinc_chlorate" title="Zinc chlorate"><span class="chemf nowrap">Zn(ClO<sub>3</sub>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>, sulfate <a href="/wiki/Zinc_sulfate" title="Zinc sulfate"><span class="chemf nowrap">ZnSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a>, phosphate <a href="/wiki/Zinc_phosphate" title="Zinc phosphate"><span class="chemf nowrap">Zn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>, molybdate <a href="/wiki/Zinc_molybdate" title="Zinc molybdate"><span class="chemf nowrap">ZnMoO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a>, cyanide <a href="/wiki/Zinc_cyanide" title="Zinc cyanide"><span class="chemf nowrap">Zn(CN)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>, arsenite <span class="chemf nowrap">Zn(AsO<sub>2</sub>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, arsenate <span class="chemf nowrap">Zn(AsO<sub>4</sub>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>·8H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> and the chromate <a href="/wiki/Zinc_chromate" title="Zinc chromate"><span class="chemf nowrap">ZnCrO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a> (one of the few colored zinc compounds) are a few examples of other common inorganic compounds of zinc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perry_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-perry-51">[51]</a></sup> One of the simplest examples of an <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compound</a> of zinc is the acetate (<a href="/wiki/Zinc_acetate" title="Zinc acetate"><span class="chemf nowrap">Zn(O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CCH<sub>3</sub>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Organozinc_compound" title="Organozinc compound">Organozinc compounds</a> are those that contain zinc–carbon covalent bonds. Diethylzinc (<a href="/wiki/Diethylzinc" title="Diethylzinc"><span class="chemf nowrap">(C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>H<sub>5</sub>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Zn</span></a>) is a reagent in synthetic chemistry. It was first reported in 1848 from the reaction of zinc and <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_iodide" title="Ethyl iodide">ethyl iodide</a>, and was the first compound known to contain a metal–carbon <a href="/wiki/Sigma_bond" title="Sigma bond">sigma bond</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Test_for_zinc">Test for zinc</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Test for zinc">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cobalticyanide paper (Rinnmann's test for Zn) can be used as a chemical indicator for zinc. 4 g of K<sub>3</sub>Co(CN)<sub>6</sub> and 1 g of KClO<sub>3</sub> is dissolved on 100 ml of water. Paper is dipped in the solution and dried at 100 °C. One drop of the sample is dropped onto the dry paper and heated.  A green disc indicates the presence of zinc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_use">Ancient use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ancient use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hemmoorer_Eimer.jpg"><img alt="Large black bowl-shaped bucket on a stand. The bucket has incrustation around its top." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="705" data-file-width="582" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Hemmoorer_Eimer.jpg/170px-Hemmoorer_Eimer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Hemmoorer_Eimer.jpg/255px-Hemmoorer_Eimer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Hemmoorer_Eimer.jpg/340px-Hemmoorer_Eimer.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hemmoorer_Eimer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Late Roman brass bucket – the <a href="/wiki/Hemmoor" title="Hemmoor">Hemmoorer</a> Eimer from Warstade, Germany, second to third century AD</div></div></div>
<p>Various isolated examples of the use of impure zinc in ancient times have been discovered. Zinc ores were used to make the zinc–copper alloy <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brass</a> thousands of years prior to the discovery of zinc as a separate element. Judean brass from the 14th to 10th centuries BC contains 23% zinc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p1201_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p1201-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Knowledge of how to produce brass spread to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a> by the 7th century BC, but few varieties were made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jas5_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-jas5-5">[5]</a></sup> Ornaments made of <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> containing 80–90% zinc, with lead, iron, <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>, and other metals making up the remainder, have been found that are 2,500 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehto1968p822_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lehto1968p822-19">[19]</a></sup>  A possibly prehistoric statuette containing 87.5% zinc was found in a <a href="/wiki/Dacia" title="Dacia">Dacian</a> archaeological site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks1933p20_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks1933p20-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest known pills were made of the zinc carbonates hydrozincite and smithsonite. The pills were used for sore eyes and were found aboard the Roman ship <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Relitto_del_Pozzino&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Relitto del Pozzino (page does not exist)">Relitto del Pozzino</a>, wrecked in 140 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>The manufacture of brass was known to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> by about 30 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p501_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p501-57">[57]</a></sup> They made brass by heating powdered <a href="/wiki/Calamine_(mineral)" title="Calamine (mineral)">calamine</a> (zinc <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> or carbonate), charcoal and copper together in a crucible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p501_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p501-57">[57]</a></sup> The resulting <a href="/wiki/Calamine_brass" title="Calamine brass">calamine brass</a> was then either cast or hammered into shape for use in weaponry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Some coins struck by Romans in the Christian era are made of what is probably calamine brass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chambers1901p799_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chambers1901p799-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> writing in the 1st century BC (but quoting a now lost work of the 4th century BC historian <a href="/wiki/Theopompus" title="Theopompus">Theopompus</a>) mentions "drops of false silver" which when mixed with copper make brass. This may refer to small quantities of zinc that is a by-product of smelting <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfide</a> ores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Zinc in such remnants in smelting ovens was usually discarded as it was thought to be worthless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks1933p21_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks1933p21-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berne_zinc_tablet" title="Berne zinc tablet">Berne zinc tablet</a> is a votive plaque dating to <a href="/wiki/Roman_Gaul" title="Roman Gaul">Roman Gaul</a> made of an alloy that is mostly zinc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Charaka_Samhita" title="Charaka Samhita">Charaka Samhita</a>, thought to have been written between 300 and 500 AD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>  mentions a metal which, when oxidized, produces <i>pushpanjan</i>, thought to be zinc oxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Zinc mines at Zawar, near <a href="/wiki/Udaipur" title="Udaipur">Udaipur</a> in India, have been active since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mauryan_period" title="Mauryan period">Mauryan period</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">  322</span> and 187 BCE). The smelting of metallic zinc here, however, appears to have begun around the 12th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ammraja_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ammraja-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craddock_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Craddock-66">[66]</a></sup> One estimate is that this location produced an estimated million tonnes of metallic zinc and zinc oxide from the 12th to 16th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p502_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p502-21">[21]</a></sup> Another estimate gives a total production of 60,000 tonnes of metallic zinc over this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ammraja_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-ammraja-65">[65]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rasaratna_Samuccaya" title="Rasaratna Samuccaya">Rasaratna Samuccaya</a>, written in approximately the 13th century AD, mentions two types of zinc-containing ores: one used for metal extraction and another used for medicinal purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craddock_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Craddock-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_studies_and_naming">Early studies and naming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Early studies and naming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Zinc was distinctly recognized as a metal under the designation of <i>Yasada</i> or Jasada in the medical Lexicon ascribed to the Hindu king Madanapala (of Taka dynasty) and written about the year 1374.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ray1903_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ray1903-67">[67]</a></sup> Smelting and extraction of impure zinc by reducing calamine with wool and other organic substances was accomplished in the 13th century in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCp4-41_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-CRCp4-41-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iza_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-iza-68">[68]</a></sup> The Chinese did not learn of the technique until the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iza_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-iza-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zinc-alchemy_symbols.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="640" height="69" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Zinc-alchemy_symbols.png/220px-Zinc-alchemy_symbols.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Zinc-alchemy_symbols.png/330px-Zinc-alchemy_symbols.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Zinc-alchemy_symbols.png/440px-Zinc-alchemy_symbols.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zinc-alchemy_symbols.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Various <a href="/wiki/Alchemical_symbol" title="Alchemical symbol">alchemical symbols</a> for the element zinc</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">Alchemists</a> burned zinc metal in air and collected the resulting zinc oxide on a <a href="/wiki/Condenser_(heat_transfer)" title="Condenser (heat transfer)">condenser</a>. Some alchemists called this zinc oxide <i>lana philosophica</i>, Latin for "philosopher's wool", because it collected in wooly tufts, whereas others thought it looked like white snow and named it <i>nix album</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>The name of the metal was probably first documented by <a href="/wiki/Paracelsus" title="Paracelsus">Paracelsus</a>, a Swiss-born German alchemist, who referred to the metal as "zincum" or "zinken" in his book <i>Liber Mineralium II</i>, in the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iza_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-iza-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The word is probably derived from the German <i lang="de" title="German language text">zinke</i>, and supposedly meant "tooth-like, pointed or jagged" (metallic zinc crystals have a needle-like appearance).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> <i>Zink</i> could also imply "tin-like" because of its relation to German <i>zinn</i> meaning tin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Yet another possibility is that the word is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> word <span dir="rtl" lang="fa" title="Persian language text">سنگ</span> <i>seng</i> meaning stone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The metal was also called Indian tin, tutanego, calamine, and spinter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehto1968p822_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lehto1968p822-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>German metallurgist <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Libavius" title="Andreas Libavius">Andreas Libavius</a> received a quantity of what he called "calay" of Malabar from a cargo ship captured from the Portuguese in 1596.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Libavius described the properties of the sample, which may have been zinc. Zinc was regularly imported to Europe from the Orient in the 17th and early 18th centuries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iza_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-iza-68">[68]</a></sup> but was at times very expensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[note 1]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Andreas_Sigismund_Marggraf-flip.jpg"><img alt="Picture of an old man head (profile). The man has a long face, short hair and tall forehead." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="433" data-file-width="326" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Andreas_Sigismund_Marggraf-flip.jpg/170px-Andreas_Sigismund_Marggraf-flip.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Andreas_Sigismund_Marggraf-flip.jpg/255px-Andreas_Sigismund_Marggraf-flip.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/85/Andreas_Sigismund_Marggraf-flip.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Andreas_Sigismund_Marggraf-flip.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Andreas_Sigismund_Marggraf" title="Andreas Sigismund Marggraf">Andreas Sigismund Marggraf</a> is given credit for first isolating pure zinc</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isolation">Isolation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Isolation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Metallic zinc was isolated in India by 1300 AD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> much earlier than in the West. Before it was isolated in Europe, it was imported from India in about 1600 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zinc-eng_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-zinc-eng-79">[78]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postlewayt" title="Postlewayt">Postlewayt's</a> <i>Universal Dictionary</i>, a contemporary source giving technological information in Europe, did not mention zinc before 1751 but the element was studied before then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craddock_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-Craddock-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Flemish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metallurgist" title="Metallurgist">metallurgist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alchemist" title="Alchemist">alchemist</a> <a href="/wiki/P._M._de_Respour" title="P. M. de Respour">P. M. de Respour</a> reported that he had extracted metallic zinc from zinc oxide in 1668.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p502_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p502-21">[21]</a></sup> By the start of the 18th century, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Fran%C3%A7ois_Geoffroy" title="Étienne François Geoffroy">Étienne François Geoffroy</a> described how zinc oxide condenses as yellow crystals on bars of iron placed above zinc ore that is being smelted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p502_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p502-21">[21]</a></sup> In Britain, <a href="/wiki/John_Lane_(metallurgist)" title="John Lane (metallurgist)">John Lane</a> is said to have carried out experiments to smelt zinc, probably at <a href="/wiki/Landore" title="Landore">Landore</a>, prior to his bankruptcy in 1726.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1738 in Great Britain, <a href="/wiki/William_Champion_(metallurgist)" title="William Champion (metallurgist)">William Champion</a> patented a process to extract zinc from calamine in a vertical <a href="/wiki/Retort" title="Retort">retort</a> style smelter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> His technique resembled that used at Zawar zinc mines in <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a>, but no evidence suggests he visited the Orient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zinc-eng_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-zinc-eng-79">[78]</a></sup> Champion's process was used through 1851.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iza_68-4"><a href="#cite_note-iza-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>German chemist <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Sigismund_Marggraf" title="Andreas Sigismund Marggraf">Andreas Marggraf</a> normally gets credit for discovering pure metallic zinc, even though Swedish chemist Anton von Swab had distilled zinc from calamine four years previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iza_68-5"><a href="#cite_note-iza-68">[68]</a></sup> In his 1746 experiment, Marggraf heated a mixture of calamine and charcoal in a closed vessel without copper to obtain a metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks1933p21_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks1933p21-61">[61]</a></sup> This procedure became commercially practical by 1752.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_work">Later work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Later work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luigi_Galvani,_oil-painting.jpg"><img alt="Painting of a middle-aged man sitting by the table, wearing a wig, black jacket, white shirt and white scarf." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="875" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Luigi_Galvani%2C_oil-painting.jpg/170px-Luigi_Galvani%2C_oil-painting.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Luigi_Galvani%2C_oil-painting.jpg/255px-Luigi_Galvani%2C_oil-painting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Luigi_Galvani%2C_oil-painting.jpg/340px-Luigi_Galvani%2C_oil-painting.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luigi_Galvani,_oil-painting.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Galvanization" title="Galvanization">Galvanization</a> was named after <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Galvani" title="Luigi Galvani">Luigi Galvani</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>William Champion's brother, John, patented a process in 1758 for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcining" title="Calcining">calcining</a> zinc sulfide into an oxide usable in the retort process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehto1968p822_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lehto1968p822-19">[19]</a></sup> Prior to this, only calamine could be used to produce zinc. In 1798, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christian_Ruberg" title="Johann Christian Ruberg">Johann Christian Ruberg</a> improved on the smelting process by building the first horizontal retort smelter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Daniel_Dony" title="Jean-Jacques Daniel Dony">Jean-Jacques Daniel Dony</a> built a different kind of horizontal zinc smelter in Belgium that processed even more zinc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iza_68-6"><a href="#cite_note-iza-68">[68]</a></sup>
Italian doctor <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Galvani" title="Luigi Galvani">Luigi Galvani</a> discovered in 1780 that connecting the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a> of a freshly dissected frog to an iron rail attached by a brass hook caused the frog's leg to twitch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ExcelPhysics_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-ExcelPhysics-85">[84]</a></sup> He incorrectly thought he had discovered an ability of nerves and muscles to create <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> and called the effect "<a href="/wiki/Bioelectricity" title="Bioelectricity">animal electricity</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IntEncyl_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-IntEncyl-86">[85]</a></sup> The galvanic cell and the process of galvanization were both named for Luigi Galvani, and his discoveries paved the way for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">electrical batteries</a>, galvanization, and <a href="/wiki/Cathodic_protection" title="Cathodic protection">cathodic protection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IntEncyl_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-IntEncyl-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Galvani's friend, <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">Alessandro Volta</a>, continued researching the effect and invented the <a href="/wiki/Voltaic_pile" title="Voltaic pile">Voltaic pile</a> in 1800.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ExcelPhysics_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-ExcelPhysics-85">[84]</a></sup> The basic unit of Volta's pile was a simplified <a href="/wiki/Galvanic_cell" title="Galvanic cell">galvanic cell</a>, made of plates of copper and zinc separated by an <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolyte</a> and connected by a conductor externally. The units were stacked in series to make the Voltaic cell, which produced electricity by directing <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> from the zinc to the copper and allowing the zinc to corrode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ExcelPhysics_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-ExcelPhysics-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The non-magnetic character of zinc and its lack of color in solution delayed discovery of its importance to biochemistry and nutrition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotton1999p626_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton1999p626-87">[86]</a></sup> This changed in 1940 when <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_anhydrase" title="Carbonic anhydrase">carbonic anhydrase</a>, an enzyme that scrubs carbon dioxide from blood, was shown to have zinc in its <a href="/wiki/Active_site" title="Active site">active site</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotton1999p626_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton1999p626-87">[86]</a></sup> The digestive enzyme <a href="/wiki/Carboxypeptidase" title="Carboxypeptidase">carboxypeptidase</a> became the second known zinc-containing enzyme in 1955.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotton1999p626_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton1999p626-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_Zinc_Production_2006.svg"><img alt="World map revealing that about 40% of zinc is produced in China, 20% in Australia, 20% in Peru, and 5% in US, Canada and Kazakhstan each." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/World_Zinc_Production_2006.svg/350px-World_Zinc_Production_2006.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/World_Zinc_Production_2006.svg/525px-World_Zinc_Production_2006.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/World_Zinc_Production_2006.svg/700px-World_Zinc_Production_2006.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_Zinc_Production_2006.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Percentage of zinc output in 2006 by countries<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSCS2008_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCS2008-88">[87]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/w/index.php?title=File:Zinc_world_production.svg&amp;lang=en"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="750" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Zinc_world_production.svg/220px-Zinc_world_production.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Zinc_world_production.svg/330px-Zinc_world_production.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Zinc_world_production.svg/440px-Zinc_world_production.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zinc_world_production.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World production trend</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zink_Mine_Rosh_Pinah.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Zink_Mine_Rosh_Pinah.jpg/220px-Zink_Mine_Rosh_Pinah.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Zink_Mine_Rosh_Pinah.jpg/330px-Zink_Mine_Rosh_Pinah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Zink_Mine_Rosh_Pinah.jpg/440px-Zink_Mine_Rosh_Pinah.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zink_Mine_Rosh_Pinah.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Zinc Mine Rosh Pinah, Namibia<br/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Zinc&amp;params=27_57_17_S_016_46_00_E_region:NA_type:landmark&amp;title=Rosh+Pinah"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">27°57′17″S</span> <span class="longitude">016°46′00″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">27.95472°S 16.76667°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">-27.95472; 16.76667</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Rosh Pinah</span>)</span></span></span></a></span></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skorpion_Zink_Mine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Skorpion_Zink_Mine.jpg/220px-Skorpion_Zink_Mine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Skorpion_Zink_Mine.jpg/330px-Skorpion_Zink_Mine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Skorpion_Zink_Mine.jpg/440px-Skorpion_Zink_Mine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skorpion_Zink_Mine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Zinc Mine Skorpion, Namibia<br/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Zinc&amp;params=27_49_09_S_016_36_28_E_region:NA_type:landmark&amp;title=Skorpion"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">27°49′09″S</span> <span class="longitude">016°36′28″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">27.81917°S 16.60778°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">-27.81917; 16.60778</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Skorpion</span>)</span></span></span></a></span></div></div></div>
<p>Zinc is the fourth most common metal in use, trailing only <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> with an annual production of about 13 million tonnes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSMCS2015_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-USGSMCS2015-22">[22]</a></sup> The world's largest zinc producer is <a href="/wiki/Nyrstar" title="Nyrstar">Nyrstar</a>, a merger of the Australian <a href="/wiki/OZ_Minerals" title="OZ Minerals">OZ Minerals</a> and the Belgian <a href="/wiki/Umicore" title="Umicore">Umicore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> About 70% of the world's zinc originates from mining, while the remaining 30% comes from recycling secondary zinc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> Commercially pure zinc is known as Special High Grade, often abbreviated <i>SHG</i>, and is 99.995% pure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup>
