</p><p>Worldwide, 95% of new zinc is mined from <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfidic</a> ore deposits, in which sphalerite (ZnS) is nearly always mixed with the sulfides of copper, lead and iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinchand_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zinchand-92">[91]</a></sup> Zinc mines are scattered throughout the world, with the main areas being China, Australia, and Peru. China produced 38% of the global zinc output in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSMCS2015_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-USGSMCS2015-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zinc metal is produced using <a href="/wiki/Extractive_metallurgy" title="Extractive metallurgy">extractive metallurgy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosenqvist1922_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenqvist1922-93">[92]</a></sup> The ore is finely ground, then put through <a href="/wiki/Froth_flotation" title="Froth flotation">froth flotation</a> to separate minerals from <a href="/wiki/Gangue" title="Gangue">gangue</a> (on the property of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobicity" title="Hydrophobicity">hydrophobicity</a>), to get a zinc sulfide ore concentrate<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosenqvist1922_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenqvist1922-93">[92]</a></sup> consisting of about 50% zinc, 32% sulfur, 13% iron, and 5% <span class="chemf nowrap">SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosenqvist1922_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenqvist1922-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Roasting_(metallurgy)" title="Roasting (metallurgy)">Roasting</a> converts the zinc sulfide concentrate to zinc oxide:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinchand_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zinchand-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 ZnS + 3 <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> → 2 ZnO + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The sulfur dioxide is used for the production of sulfuric acid, which is necessary for the leaching process. If deposits of zinc carbonate, zinc silicate, or zinc spinel (like the <a href="/wiki/Skorpion_Zinc" title="Skorpion Zinc">Skorpion Deposit</a> in Namibia) are used for zinc production, the roasting can be omitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skorpion_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Skorpion-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>For further processing two basic methods are used: <a href="/wiki/Pyrometallurgy" title="Pyrometallurgy">pyrometallurgy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Electrowinning" title="Electrowinning">electrowinning</a>. Pyrometallurgy reduces zinc oxide with <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> at 950 °C (1,740 °F) into the metal, which is distilled as zinc vapor to separate it from other metals, which are not volatile at those temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> The zinc vapor is collected in a condenser.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinchand_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zinchand-92">[91]</a></sup> The equations below describe this process:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinchand_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-Zinchand-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 ZnO + C → 2 Zn + <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd>
<dd>ZnO + CO → Zn + <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Electrowinning" title="Electrowinning">electrowinning</a>, zinc is leached from the ore concentrate by <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>ZnO + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">ZnSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></dd></dl>
<p>Finally, the zinc is reduced by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinchand_92-4"><a href="#cite_note-Zinchand-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">ZnSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → 2 Zn + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The sulfuric acid is regenerated and recycled to the leaching step.
</p><p>When galvanised feedstock is fed to an <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc_furnace" title="Electric arc furnace">electric arc furnace</a>, the zinc is recovered from the dust by a number of processes, predominately the <a href="/wiki/Waelz_process" title="Waelz process">Waelz process</a> (90% as of 2014).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Environmental impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Refinement of sulfidic zinc ores produces large volumes of sulfur dioxide and <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a> vapor. Smelter <a href="/wiki/Slag" title="Slag">slag</a> and other residues contain significant quantities of metals. About 1.1 million tonnes of metallic zinc and 130 thousand tonnes of lead were mined and smelted in the Belgian towns of <a href="/wiki/Kelmis" title="Kelmis">La Calamine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plombi%C3%A8res" title="Plombières">Plombières</a> between 1806 and 1882.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kucha_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kucha-98">[97]</a></sup> The dumps of the past mining operations leach zinc and cadmium, and the sediments of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geul_River" title="Geul River">Geul River</a> contain non-trivial amounts of metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kucha_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kucha-98">[97]</a></sup> About two thousand years ago, emissions of zinc from mining and smelting totaled 10 thousand tonnes a year. After increasing 10-fold from 1850, zinc emissions peaked at 3.4 million tonnes per year in the 1980s and declined to 2.7 million tonnes in the 1990s, although a 2005 study of the Arctic troposphere found that the concentrations there did not reflect the decline. Anthropogenic and natural emissions occur at a ratio of 20 to 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broadley2007_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Broadley2007-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zinc in rivers flowing through industrial and mining areas can be as high as 20 ppm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p504_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p504-100">[99]</a></sup> Effective <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">sewage treatment</a> greatly reduces this; treatment along the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a>, for example, has decreased zinc levels to 50 ppb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p504_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p504-100">[99]</a></sup> Concentrations of zinc as low as 2 ppm adversely affects the amount of oxygen that fish can carry in their blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:1158px;">
<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Zinc_Works_and_Incat.jpg" title="Historically responsible for high metal levels in the Derwent River, the zinc works at Lutana is the largest exporter in Tasmania, generating 2.5% of the state's GDP, and producing more than 250 000 tonnes of zinc per year."><img alt="A panorama featuring a large industrial plant on a sea side, in front of mountains." data-file-height="1462" data-file-width="11034" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/The_Zinc_Works_and_Incat.jpg/1150px-The_Zinc_Works_and_Incat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/The_Zinc_Works_and_Incat.jpg/1725px-The_Zinc_Works_and_Incat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/The_Zinc_Works_and_Incat.jpg/2300px-The_Zinc_Works_and_Incat.jpg 2x" width="1150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Zinc_Works_and_Incat.jpg" title="File:The Zinc Works and Incat.jpg"> </a></div>Historically responsible for high metal levels in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Derwent_River_(Tasmania)" title="Derwent River (Tasmania)">Derwent River</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> the zinc works at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutana" title="Lutana">Lutana</a> is the largest exporter in Tasmania, generating 2.5% of the state's <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a>, and producing more than 250 000 tonnes of zinc per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup></div>
</div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Soil_contamination" title="Soil contamination">Soils contaminated</a> with zinc from mining, refining, or fertilizing with zinc-bearing sludge can contain several grams of zinc per kilogram of dry soil. Levels of zinc in excess of 500 ppm in soil interfere with the ability of plants to absorb other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_mineral" title="Dietary mineral">essential metals</a>, such as iron and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>. Zinc levels of 2000 ppm to 180,000 ppm (18%) have been recorded in some soil samples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p504_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p504-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Major applications of zinc include (numbers are given for the US)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS-yb2006_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-yb2006-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Galvanization" title="Galvanization">Galvanizing</a> (55%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">Brass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> (16%)</li>
<li>Other alloys (21%)</li>
<li>Miscellaneous (8%)</li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-corrosion_and_batteries">Anti-corrosion and batteries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Anti-corrosion and batteries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Feuerverzinkte_Oberfl%C3%A4che.jpg"><img alt="Merged elongated crystals of various shades of gray." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Feuerverzinkte_Oberfl%C3%A4che.jpg/220px-Feuerverzinkte_Oberfl%C3%A4che.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Feuerverzinkte_Oberfl%C3%A4che.jpg/330px-Feuerverzinkte_Oberfl%C3%A4che.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Feuerverzinkte_Oberfl%C3%A4che.jpg/440px-Feuerverzinkte_Oberfl%C3%A4che.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Feuerverzinkte_Oberfl%C3%A4che.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hot-dip handrail <a href="/wiki/Galvanization" title="Galvanization">galvanized</a> crystalline surface</div></div></div>
<p>Zinc is most commonly used as an anti-<a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a> agent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p1203_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p1203-105">[104]</a></sup> and galvanization (coating of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> or <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>) is the most familiar form. In 2009 in the United States, 55% or 893,000 tons of the zinc metal was used for galvanization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS-yb2006_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-yb2006-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zinc is more reactive than iron or steel and thus will attract almost all local oxidation until it completely corrodes away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stwertka1998p99_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stwertka1998p99-106">[105]</a></sup> A protective surface layer of oxide and carbonate (<span class="chemf nowrap">Zn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>(CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>)</span> forms as the zinc corrodes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehto1968p829_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lehto1968p829-107">[106]</a></sup> This protection lasts even after the zinc layer is scratched but degrades through time as the zinc corrodes away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehto1968p829_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lehto1968p829-107">[106]</a></sup> The zinc is applied electrochemically or as molten zinc by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot-dip_galvanizing" title="Hot-dip galvanizing">hot-dip galvanizing</a> or spraying. Galvanization is used on chain-link fencing, guard rails, suspension bridges, lightposts, metal roofs, heat exchangers, and car bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p503_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p503-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The relative reactivity of zinc and its ability to attract oxidation to itself makes it an efficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacrificial_anode" title="Sacrificial anode">sacrificial anode</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cathodic_protection" title="Cathodic protection">cathodic protection</a> (CP). For example, cathodic protection of a buried pipeline can be achieved by connecting anodes made from zinc to the pipe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehto1968p829_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lehto1968p829-107">[106]</a></sup> Zinc acts as the <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anode</a> (negative terminus) by slowly corroding away as it passes electric current to the steel pipeline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehto1968p829_107-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lehto1968p829-107">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[note 2]</a></sup> Zinc is also used to cathodically protect metals that are exposed to sea water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup> A zinc disc attached to a ship's iron rudder will slowly corrode while the rudder stays intact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stwertka1998p99_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stwertka1998p99-106">[105]</a></sup> Similarly, a zinc plug attached to a propeller or the metal protective guard for the keel of the ship provides temporary protection.
</p><p>With a <a href="/wiki/Standard_electrode_potential" title="Standard electrode potential">standard electrode potential</a> (SEP) of −0.76 <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">volts</a>, zinc is used as an anode material for batteries. (More reactive lithium (SEP −3.04 V) is used for anodes in <a href="/wiki/Lithium_battery" title="Lithium battery">lithium batteries</a> ). Powdered zinc is used in this way in <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_battery" title="Alkaline battery">alkaline batteries</a> and the case (which also serves as the anode) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc-carbon_battery" title="Zinc-carbon battery">zinc–carbon batteries</a> is formed from sheet zinc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup> Zinc is used as the anode or fuel of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc-air_battery" title="Zinc-air battery">zinc-air battery</a>/fuel cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Zinc%E2%80%93cerium_battery" title="Zinc–cerium battery">zinc-cerium</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Redox_flow_battery" title="Redox flow battery">redox flow battery</a> also relies on a zinc-based negative half-cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xie1_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xie1-115">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alloys">Alloys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Alloys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A widely used zinc alloy is brass, in which copper is alloyed with anywhere from 3% to 45% zinc, depending upon the type of brass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehto1968p829_107-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lehto1968p829-107">[106]</a></sup> Brass is generally more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ductile" title="Ductile">ductile</a> and stronger than copper, and has superior <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corrosion_resistance" title="Corrosion resistance">corrosion resistance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehto1968p829_107-5"><a href="#cite_note-Lehto1968p829-107">[106]</a></sup> These properties make it useful in communication equipment, hardware, musical instruments, and water valves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehto1968p829_107-6"><a href="#cite_note-Lehto1968p829-107">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Microstructure_of_rolled_and_annealed_brass;_magnification_400X.jpg"><img alt="A mosaica pattern composed of components having various shapes and shades of brown." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Microstructure_of_rolled_and_annealed_brass%3B_magnification_400X.jpg/220px-Microstructure_of_rolled_and_annealed_brass%3B_magnification_400X.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Microstructure_of_rolled_and_annealed_brass%3B_magnification_400X.jpg/330px-Microstructure_of_rolled_and_annealed_brass%3B_magnification_400X.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Microstructure_of_rolled_and_annealed_brass%3B_magnification_400X.jpg/440px-Microstructure_of_rolled_and_annealed_brass%3B_magnification_400X.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Microstructure_of_rolled_and_annealed_brass;_magnification_400X.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cast brass microstructure at magnification 400x</div></div></div>
<p>Other widely used zinc alloys include <a href="/wiki/Nickel_silver" title="Nickel silver">nickel silver</a>, typewriter metal, soft and aluminium <a href="/wiki/Solder" title="Solder">solder</a>, and commercial <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCp4-41_12-5"><a href="#cite_note-CRCp4-41-12">[12]</a></sup> Zinc is also used in contemporary pipe organs as a substitute for the traditional lead/tin alloy in pipes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup> Alloys of 85–88% zinc, 4–10% copper, and 2–8% aluminium find limited use in certain types of machine bearings. Zinc is the primary metal in <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_cent" title="Lincoln cent">American one cent coins</a> (pennies) since 1982.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onecent_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-onecent-117">[115]</a></sup> The zinc core is coated with a thin layer of copper to give the appearance of a copper coin. In 1994, 33,200 tonnes (36,600 short tons) of zinc were used to produce 13.6 billion pennies in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS-yb1994_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-yb1994-118">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alloys of zinc with small amounts of copper, aluminium, and magnesium are useful in <a href="/wiki/Die_casting" title="Die casting">die casting</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Spin_casting" title="Spin casting">spin casting</a>, especially in the automotive, electrical, and hardware industries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCp4-41_12-6"><a href="#cite_note-CRCp4-41-12">[12]</a></sup> These alloys are marketed under the name <a href="/wiki/Zamak" title="Zamak">Zamak</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[117]</a></sup> An example of this is <a href="/wiki/Zinc_aluminium" title="Zinc aluminium">zinc aluminium</a>. The low melting point together with the low <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> of the alloy makes possible the production of small and intricate shapes. The low working temperature leads to rapid cooling of the cast products and fast production for assembly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCp4-41_12-7"><a href="#cite_note-CRCp4-41-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup> Another alloy, marketed under the brand name Prestal, contains 78% zinc and 22% aluminium, and is reported to be nearly as strong as steel but as malleable as plastic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCp4-41_12-8"><a href="#cite_note-CRCp4-41-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Superplasticity" title="Superplasticity">superplasticity</a> of the alloy allows it to be molded using die casts made of ceramics and cement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCp4-41_12-9"><a href="#cite_note-CRCp4-41-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Similar alloys with the addition of a small amount of lead can be cold-rolled into sheets. An alloy of 96% zinc and 4% aluminium is used to make stamping dies for low production run applications for which ferrous metal dies would be too expensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-samans_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-samans-122">[120]</a></sup> For building facades, roofing, and other applications for <a href="/wiki/Sheet_metal" title="Sheet metal">sheet metal</a> formed by <a href="/wiki/Deep_drawing" title="Deep drawing">deep drawing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roll_forming" title="Roll forming">roll forming</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Bending_(metalworking)" title="Bending (metalworking)">bending</a>, zinc alloys with <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> and copper are used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZincCorr_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZincCorr-123">[121]</a></sup> Unalloyed zinc is too brittle for these manufacturing processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZincCorr_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-ZincCorr-123">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a dense, inexpensive, easily worked material, zinc is used as a <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> replacement. In the wake of <a href="/wiki/Lead_poisoning" title="Lead poisoning">lead concerns</a>, zinc appears in weights for various applications ranging from fishing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Tire_balance" title="Tire balance">tire balances</a> and flywheels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minrecall_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-minrecall-125">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cadmium_zinc_telluride" title="Cadmium zinc telluride">Cadmium zinc telluride</a> (CZT) is a <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductive</a> alloy that can be divided into an array of small sensing devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Katz2002_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Katz2002-126">[124]</a></sup> These devices are similar to an <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">integrated circuit</a> and can detect the energy of incoming <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a> photons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Katz2002_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-Katz2002-126">[124]</a></sup> When behind an absorbing mask, the CZT sensor array can determine the direction of the rays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Katz2002_126-2"><a href="#cite_note-Katz2002-126">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_industrial_uses">Other industrial uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other industrial uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zinc_oxide.jpg"><img alt="White powder on a glass plate" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="496" data-file-width="588" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Zinc_oxide.jpg/220px-Zinc_oxide.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Zinc_oxide.jpg/330px-Zinc_oxide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Zinc_oxide.jpg/440px-Zinc_oxide.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zinc_oxide.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Zinc oxide is used as a white <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paint" title="Paint">paints</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Roughly one quarter of all zinc output in the United States in 2009 was consumed in zinc compounds;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS-yb2006_104-2"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-yb2006-104">[103]</a></sup> a variety of which are used industrially. Zinc oxide is widely used as a white pigment in paints and as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> in the manufacture of rubber to disburse heat. Zinc oxide is used to protect rubber polymers and plastics from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation" title="Ultraviolet radiation">ultraviolet radiation</a> (UV).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p503_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p503-18">[18]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> properties of zinc oxide make it useful in <a href="/wiki/Varistor" title="Varistor">varistors</a> and photocopying products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_zinc-oxide_cycle" title="Zinc zinc-oxide cycle">zinc zinc-oxide cycle</a> is a two step <a href="/wiki/Thermochemistry" title="Thermochemistry">thermochemical</a> process based on zinc and zinc oxide for <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_production" title="Hydrogen production">hydrogen production</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Zinc_chloride" title="Zinc chloride">Zinc chloride</a> is often added to lumber as a <a href="/wiki/Fire_retardant" title="Fire retardant">fire retardant</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heiserman1992p124_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heiserman1992p124-129">[127]</a></sup> and sometimes as a wood <a href="/wiki/Preservative" title="Preservative">preservative</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup> It is used in the manufacture of other chemicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heiserman1992p124_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Heiserman1992p124-129">[127]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_methyl" title="Zinc methyl">Zinc methyl</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Zn(CH<sub>3</sub>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) is used in a number of organic <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">syntheses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zinc_sulfide" title="Zinc sulfide">Zinc sulfide</a> (ZnS) is used in <a href="/wiki/Luminescence" title="Luminescence">luminescent</a> pigments such as on the hands of clocks, <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> and television screens, and <a href="/wiki/Luminous_paint" title="Luminous paint">luminous paints</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCp4-42_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRCp4-42-132">[130]</a></sup> Crystals of ZnS are used in <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">lasers</a> that operate in the mid-<a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> part of the spectrum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zinc_sulfate" title="Zinc sulfate">Zinc sulfate</a> is a chemical in <a href="/wiki/Dye" title="Dye">dyes</a> and pigments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heiserman1992p124_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-Heiserman1992p124-129">[127]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zinc_pyrithione" title="Zinc pyrithione">Zinc pyrithione</a> is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antifouling" title="Antifouling">antifouling</a> paints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zinc powder is sometimes used as a <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">propellant</a> in <a href="/wiki/Model_rocket" title="Model rocket">model rockets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZnS_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZnS-135">[133]</a></sup> When a compressed mixture of 70% zinc and 30% <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> powder is ignited there is a violent chemical reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZnS_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-ZnS-135">[133]</a></sup> This produces zinc sulfide, together with large amounts of hot gas, heat, and light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZnS_135-2"><a href="#cite_note-ZnS-135">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zinc sheet metal is used to make zinc <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bar_(counter)" title="Bar (counter)">bars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">64</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span>, the most abundant isotope of zinc, is very susceptible to <a href="/wiki/Neutron_activation" title="Neutron activation">neutron activation</a>, being <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmuted</a> into the highly radioactive <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">65</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span>, which has a half-life of 244 days and produces intense <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma radiation</a>. Because of this, zinc oxide used in nuclear reactors as an anti-corrosion agent is depleted of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">64</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span> before use, this is called <a href="/wiki/Depleted_zinc_oxide" title="Depleted zinc oxide">depleted zinc oxide</a>. For the same reason, zinc has been proposed as a <a href="/wiki/Salted_bomb" title="Salted bomb">salting</a> material for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> is another, better-known salting material).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Win2003_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Win2003-137">[135]</a></sup> A jacket of <a href="/wiki/Isotope_separation" title="Isotope separation">isotopically enriched</a> <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">64</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span> would be irradiated by the intense high-energy neutron flux from an exploding thermonuclear weapon, forming a large amount of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">65</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span> significantly increasing the radioactivity of the weapon's <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fallout" title="Nuclear fallout">fallout</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Win2003_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Win2003-137">[135]</a></sup> Such a weapon is not known to have ever been built, tested, or used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Win2003_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-Win2003-137">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">65</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Zn</span> is used as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotopic_tracer" title="Isotopic tracer">tracer</a> to study how alloys that contain zinc wear out, or the path and the role of zinc in organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zinc dithiocarbamate complexes are used as agricultural <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicides</a>; these include <a href="/wiki/Zineb" title="Zineb">Zineb</a>, Metiram, Propineb and Ziram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup> Zinc naphthenate is used as wood preservative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup> Zinc in the form of <a href="/wiki/Zinc_dithiophosphate" title="Zinc dithiophosphate">ZDDP</a>, is used as an anti-wear additive for metal parts in engine oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_chemistry">Organic chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Organic chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DiphenylzincCarbonylAddition.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="676" data-file-width="1037" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/DiphenylzincCarbonylAddition.png/350px-DiphenylzincCarbonylAddition.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/DiphenylzincCarbonylAddition.png/525px-DiphenylzincCarbonylAddition.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/DiphenylzincCarbonylAddition.png/700px-DiphenylzincCarbonylAddition.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DiphenylzincCarbonylAddition.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Addition of diphenylzinc to an aldehyde</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Organozinc_compound" title="Organozinc compound">Organozinc</a> chemistry is the science of compounds that contain carbon-zinc bonds, describing the physical properties, synthesis, and chemical reactions.Many organozinc compounds are important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup> Among important applications are
</p>
<ul><li>The Frankland-Duppa Reaction in which an <a href="/wiki/Oxalate" title="Oxalate">oxalate</a> <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">ester</a> (ROCOCOOR) reacts with an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkyl_halide" title="Alkyl halide">alkyl halide</a> R'X, zinc and <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> to form the α-hydroxycarboxylic esters RR'COHCOOR<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reformatskii_reaction" title="Reformatskii reaction">Reformatskii reaction</a> in which α-halo-esters and aldehydes are converted to β-hydroxy-esters</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Simmons%E2%80%93Smith_reaction" title="Simmons–Smith reaction">Simmons–Smith reaction</a> in which the carbenoid (iodomethyl)zinc iodide reacts with alkene(or alkyne) and converts them to cyclopropane</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Addition_reaction" title="Addition reaction">Addition reaction</a> of organozinc compounds to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbonyl" title="Carbonyl">carbonyl</a> compounds</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Barbier_reaction" title="Barbier reaction">Barbier reaction</a> (1899), which is the zinc equivalent of the magnesium <a href="/wiki/Grignard_reaction" title="Grignard reaction">Grignard reaction</a> and is the better of the two.  In presence of water, formation of the organomagnesium halide will fail, whereas the Barbier reaction can take place in water.</li>
<li>On the downside, organozincs are much less nucleophilic than Grignards, and they are expensive and difficult to handle. Commercially available diorganozinc compounds are <a href="/wiki/Dimethylzinc" title="Dimethylzinc">dimethylzinc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diethylzinc" title="Diethylzinc">diethylzinc</a> and diphenylzinc. In one study,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup> the active organozinc compound is obtained from much cheaper <a href="/wiki/Organobromine_compound" title="Organobromine compound">organobromine</a> precursors</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Negishi_coupling" title="Negishi coupling">Negishi coupling</a> is also an important reaction for the formation of new carbon-carbon bonds between unsaturated carbon atoms in alkenes, arenes and alkynes. The catalysts are nickel and palladium. A key step in the <a href="/wiki/Catalytic_cycle" title="Catalytic cycle">catalytic cycle</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Transmetalation" title="Transmetalation">transmetalation</a> in which a zinc halide exchanges its organic substituent for another halogen with the palladium (nickel) metal center.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Fukuyama_coupling" title="Fukuyama coupling">Fukuyama coupling</a> is another coupling reaction, but it uses a thioester as reactant and produces a ketone.</li></ul>
<p>Zinc has found many applications as catalyst in organic synthesis including asymmetric synthesis, being cheap and easily available alternative to precious metal complexes. The results (yield and <a href="/wiki/Enantiomeric_excess" title="Enantiomeric excess">ee</a>) obtained with chiral zinc catalysts are comparable to those achieved with palladium, ruthenium, iridium and others, and zinc becomes metal catalyst of choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_supplement">Dietary supplement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Dietary supplement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zinc_50_mg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1721" data-file-width="996" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Zinc_50_mg.jpg/180px-Zinc_50_mg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Zinc_50_mg.jpg/270px-Zinc_50_mg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Zinc_50_mg.jpg/360px-Zinc_50_mg.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zinc_50_mg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_Nutrition_Centers" title="General Nutrition Centers">GNC</a> zinc 50 mg tablets. The amount exceeds what is deemed the safe upper limit in the United States (40 mg) and European Union (25 mg)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zinc_gluconate_structure.svg"><img alt="Skeletal chemical formula of a planar compound featuring a Zn atom in the center, symmetrically bonded to four oxygens. Those oxygens are further connected to linear COH chains." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="111" data-file-width="338" height="72" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Zinc_gluconate_structure.svg/220px-Zinc_gluconate_structure.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Zinc_gluconate_structure.svg/330px-Zinc_gluconate_structure.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Zinc_gluconate_structure.svg/440px-Zinc_gluconate_structure.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zinc_gluconate_structure.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zinc_gluconate" title="Zinc gluconate">Zinc gluconate</a> is one compound used for the delivery of zinc as a <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">dietary supplement</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Zinc_sulfate_(medical_use)" title="Zinc sulfate (medical use)">Zinc sulfate (medical use)</a></div>
<p>In most single-tablet, over-the-counter, daily vitamin and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_mineral" title="Dietary mineral">mineral</a> supplements, zinc is included in such forms as <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide" title="Zinc oxide">zinc oxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc_acetate" title="Zinc acetate">zinc acetate</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Zinc_gluconate" title="Zinc gluconate">zinc gluconate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DiSilvestro2004_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-DiSilvestro2004-150">[148]</a></sup> Zinc is generally considered to be an antioxidant. However, it is redox inert and thus can serve such a function only indirectly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup> Generally, zinc supplement is recommended where there is high risk of zinc deficiency (such as low and middle income countries) as a preventive measure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zinc deficiency has been associated with <a href="/wiki/Major_depressive_disorder" title="Major depressive disorder">major depressive disorder</a> (MDD), and zinc supplements may be an effective treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23567517_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23567517-153">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zinc serves as a simple, inexpensive, and critical tool for treating diarrheal episodes among children in the developing world. Zinc becomes depleted in the body during <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>, but recent studies suggest that replenishing zinc with a 10- to 14-day course of treatment can reduce the duration and severity of diarrheal episodes and may also prevent future episodes for as long as three months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>A Cochrane review stated that people taking zinc supplement may be less likely to progress to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age-related_macular_degeneration" title="Age-related macular degeneration">age-related macular degeneration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zinc supplement is an effective treatment for <a href="/wiki/Acrodermatitis_enteropathica" title="Acrodermatitis enteropathica">acrodermatitis enteropathica</a>, a genetic disorder affecting zinc absorption that was previously fatal to affected infants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p501_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p501-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gastroenteritis" title="Gastroenteritis">Gastroenteritis</a> is strongly attenuated by ingestion of zinc, possibly by direct antimicrobial action of the ions in the <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">gastrointestinal tract</a>, or by the absorption of the zinc and re-release from immune cells (all <a href="/wiki/Granulocyte" title="Granulocyte">granulocytes</a> secrete zinc), or both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, researchers reported that adding large amounts of zinc to a urine sample masked detection of drugs. The researchers did not test whether orally consuming a zinc dietary supplement could have the same effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zinc is a negative allosteric modulator of the <a href="/wiki/GABAA_receptor" title="GABAA receptor">GABA<sub>A</sub> receptor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12640458_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12640458-159">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_cold">Common cold<span id="Common_cold"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Common cold">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable relarticle mainarticle selfreference noprint" role="note">This section is <a href="/wiki/Transclusion" title="Transclusion">transcluded</a> from <a href="/wiki/Zinc_and_the_common_cold" title="Zinc and the common cold">Zinc and the common cold</a>. <span class="plainlinks" style="font-style: normal"><span style="font-size:85%;">(<a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Zinc_and_the_common_cold&amp;action=edit">edit</a> | <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Zinc_and_the_common_cold&amp;action=history">history</a>)</span></span></div>
<p>Zinc supplements (frequently <a href="/wiki/Zinc_acetate" title="Zinc acetate">zinc acetate</a> or <a href="/wiki/Zinc_gluconate" title="Zinc gluconate">zinc gluconate</a> <a href="/wiki/Throat_lozenge" title="Throat lozenge">lozenges</a>) are a group of <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">dietary supplements</a> that are commonly used for the treatment of the <a href="/wiki/Common_cold" title="Common cold">common cold</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH_Zinc_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIH_Zinc-160">[158]</a></sup> The use of zinc supplements at doses in excess of 75 mg/day within 24 hours of the onset of symptoms has been shown to reduce the duration of cold symptoms by about 1 day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH_Zinc_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIH_Zinc-160">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_for_the_common_cold_–_2013_Cochrane_review_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_for_the_common_cold_–_2013_Cochrane_review-161">[159]</a></sup> Due to a lack of data, there is insufficient evidence to determine whether the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophylaxis" title="Prophylaxis">preventative use</a> of zinc supplements reduces the likelihood of contracting a cold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_for_the_common_cold_–_2013_Cochrane_review_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_for_the_common_cold_–_2013_Cochrane_review-161">[159]</a></sup> Adverse effects with zinc supplements <a href="/wiki/Oral_administration" title="Oral administration">by mouth</a> include bad taste and <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH_Zinc_160-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIH_Zinc-160">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_for_the_common_cold_–_2013_Cochrane_review_161-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_for_the_common_cold_–_2013_Cochrane_review-161">[159]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nasal_administration" title="Nasal administration">intranasal use</a> of zinc-containing <a href="/wiki/Nasal_spray" title="Nasal spray">nasal sprays</a> has been associated with the <a href="/wiki/Anosmia" title="Anosmia">loss of the sense of smell</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH_Zinc_160-3"><a href="#cite_note-NIH_Zinc-160">[158]</a></sup> consequently, in June 2009, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="United States Food and Drug Administration">United States Food and Drug Administration</a> (USFDA) warned consumers to stop using intranasal zinc products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH_Zinc_160-4"><a href="#cite_note-NIH_Zinc-160">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_rhinovirus" title="Human rhinovirus">human rhinovirus</a> – the most common <a href="/wiki/Viral_disease" title="Viral disease">viral pathogen</a> in humans – is the predominant cause of the common cold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2015Full_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2015Full-162">[160]</a></sup> The hypothesized <a href="/wiki/Mechanism_of_action" title="Mechanism of action">mechanism of action</a> by which zinc reduces the severity and/or duration of cold symptoms is the suppression of nasal <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> and the direct inhibition of <a href="/wiki/Rhinovirus#Pathogenesis" title="Rhinovirus">rhinoviral receptor binding</a> and rhinoviral <a href="/wiki/Viral_replication" title="Viral replication">replication</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Nasal_mucosa" title="Nasal mucosa">nasal mucosa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH_Zinc_160-5"><a href="#cite_note-NIH_Zinc-160">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Topical_use">Topical use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Topical use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide#Medicine" title="Zinc oxide">Zinc oxide § Medicine</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topical_administration" title="Topical administration">Topical preparations</a> of zinc include those used on the skin, often in the form of <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide" title="Zinc oxide">zinc oxide</a>. Zinc preparations can protect against <a href="/wiki/Sunburn" title="Sunburn">sunburn</a> in the summer and <a href="/wiki/Windburn" title="Windburn">windburn</a> in the winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p501_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p501-57">[57]</a></sup> Applied thinly to a baby's diaper area (<a href="/wiki/Perineum" title="Perineum">perineum</a>) with each diaper change, it can protect against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diaper_rash" title="Diaper rash">diaper rash</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p501_57-4"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p501-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chelated zinc is used in toothpastes and mouthwashes to prevent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halitosis" title="Halitosis">bad breath</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Zinc_pyrithione" title="Zinc pyrithione">Zinc pyrithione</a> is widely included in shampoos to prevent dandruff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Biological role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Zinc is an essential <a href="/wiki/Trace_element" title="Trace element">trace element</a> for humans<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_-_brain_disorders_2015_review_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_-_brain_disorders_2015_review-166">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review-167">[165]</a></sup> and other animals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[166]</a></sup> for plants<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broadley2007_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Broadley2007-99">[98]</a></sup>  and for <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[167]</a></sup> Zinc is required for the function of over 300 <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> and 1000 <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factors</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review-167">[165]</a></sup> and is stored and transferred in <a href="/wiki/Metallothionein" title="Metallothionein">metallothioneins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotton1999bio_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton1999bio-170">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[169]</a></sup> It is the second most abundant trace metal in humans after iron and it is the only metal which appears in all <a href="/wiki/Enzyme#Naming_conventions" title="Enzyme">enzyme classes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broadley2007_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-Broadley2007-99">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review_167-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review-167">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>In proteins, zinc ions are often coordinated to the amino acid side chains of <a href="/wiki/Aspartic_acid_(data_page)" title="Aspartic acid (data page)">aspartic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glutamic_acid" title="Glutamic acid">glutamic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Histidine" title="Histidine">histidine</a>. The theoretical and computational description of this zinc binding in proteins (as well as that of other transition metals) is difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>Roughly <span class="nowrap">2–4</span> grams of zinc<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rink2000_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rink2000-173">[171]</a></sup> are distributed throughout the human body. Most zinc is in the brain, muscle, bones, kidney, and liver, with the highest concentrations in the prostate and parts of the eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[172]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Semen" title="Semen">Semen</a> is particularly rich in zinc, a key factor in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prostate_gland" title="Prostate gland">prostate gland</a> function and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reproductive_organ" title="Reproductive organ">reproductive organ</a> growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berdanier2007_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berdanier2007-175">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>In humans, the biological roles of zinc are ubiquitous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hambridge2007_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hambridge2007-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_-_brain_disorders_2015_review_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_-_brain_disorders_2015_review-166">[164]</a></sup> It interacts with "a wide range of organic <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligands</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hambridge2007_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hambridge2007-9">[9]</a></sup> and has roles in the metabolism of RNA and DNA, <a href="/wiki/Signal_transduction" title="Signal transduction">signal transduction</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a>. It also regulates <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a>. A 2006 study estimated that about 10% of human proteins (2800) potentially bind zinc, in addition to hundreds more that transport and traffic zinc; a similar <i><a href="/wiki/In_silico" title="In silico">in silico</a></i> study in the plant <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> found 2367 zinc-related proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broadley2007_99-3"><a href="#cite_note-Broadley2007-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>, zinc is stored in specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synaptic_vesicles" title="Synaptic vesicles">synaptic vesicles</a> by <a href="/wiki/Glutamatergic" title="Glutamatergic">glutamatergic</a> <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> and can modulate neuronal excitability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_-_brain_disorders_2015_review_166-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_-_brain_disorders_2015_review-166">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review_167-3"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review-167">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bitanihirwe_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bitanihirwe-176">[174]</a></sup> It plays a key role in <a href="/wiki/Synaptic_plasticity" title="Synaptic plasticity">synaptic plasticity</a> and so in learning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_-_brain_disorders_2015_review_166-3"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_-_brain_disorders_2015_review-166">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[175]</a></sup> Zinc <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a> also plays a critical role in the functional regulation of the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_-_brain_disorders_2015_review_166-4"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_-_brain_disorders_2015_review-166">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bitanihirwe_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bitanihirwe-176">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review_167-4"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review-167">[165]</a></sup> Dysregulation of zinc homeostasis in the central nervous system that results in excessive synaptic zinc concentrations is believed to induce <a href="/wiki/Neurotoxicity" title="Neurotoxicity">neurotoxicity</a> through mitochondrial oxidative stress (e.g., by disrupting certain enzymes involved in the <a href="/wiki/Electron_transport_chain" title="Electron transport chain">electron transport chain</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_I" title="Complex I">complex I</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_III" title="Complex III">complex III</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%91-ketoglutarate_dehydrogenase" title="Α-ketoglutarate dehydrogenase">α-ketoglutarate dehydrogenase</a>), the dysregulation of calcium homeostasis, glutamatergic neuronal <a href="/wiki/Excitotoxicity" title="Excitotoxicity">excitotoxicity</a>, and interference with intraneuronal <a href="/wiki/Signal_transduction" title="Signal transduction">signal transduction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_-_brain_disorders_2015_review_166-5"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_-_brain_disorders_2015_review-166">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid25265815_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25265815-178">[176]</a></sup> L- and D-histidine facilitate brain zinc uptake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[177]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/SLC30A3" title="SLC30A3">SLC30A3</a> is the primary <a href="/wiki/Solute_carrier_family#Solute_carrier_family_30" title="Solute carrier family">zinc transporter</a> involved in cerebral zinc homeostasis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_-_brain_disorders_2015_review_166-6"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_-_brain_disorders_2015_review-166">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enzymes">Enzymes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Enzymes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbonic_anhydrase.png"><img alt="Interconnected stripes, mostly of yellow and blue color with a few red segments." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="558" data-file-width="611" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Carbonic_anhydrase.png/220px-Carbonic_anhydrase.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Carbonic_anhydrase.png/330px-Carbonic_anhydrase.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Carbonic_anhydrase.png/440px-Carbonic_anhydrase.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbonic_anhydrase.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ribbon_diagram" title="Ribbon diagram">Ribbon diagram</a> of human <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_anhydrase" title="Carbonic anhydrase">carbonic anhydrase</a> II, with zinc atom visible in the center</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zinc_finger_rendered.png"><img alt="A twisted band, with one side painted blue and another gray. Its two ends are connected through some chemical species to a green atom (zinc)." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1060" data-file-width="1420" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Zinc_finger_rendered.png/220px-Zinc_finger_rendered.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Zinc_finger_rendered.png/330px-Zinc_finger_rendered.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Zinc_finger_rendered.png/440px-Zinc_finger_rendered.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zinc_finger_rendered.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_fingers" title="Zinc fingers">Zinc fingers</a> help read DNA sequences.</div></div></div>
<p>Zinc is an efficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acid</a>, making it a useful catalytic agent in <a href="/wiki/Hydroxylation" title="Hydroxylation">hydroxylation</a> and other enzymatic reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC2000p443_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-NRC2000p443-180">[178]</a></sup> The metal also has a flexible <a href="/wiki/Coordination_geometry" title="Coordination geometry">coordination geometry</a>, which allows proteins using it to rapidly shift <a href="/wiki/Protein_structure" title="Protein structure">conformations</a> to perform biological reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[179]</a></sup> Two examples of zinc-containing enzymes are <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_anhydrase" title="Carbonic anhydrase">carbonic anhydrase</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carboxypeptidase" title="Carboxypeptidase">carboxypeptidase</a>, which are vital to the processes of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) regulation and digestion of proteins, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p1224—1225_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p1224—1225-182">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>In vertebrate blood, carbonic anhydrase converts <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> into bicarbonate and the same enzyme transforms the bicarbonate back into <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> for exhalation through the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[181]</a></sup> Without this enzyme, this conversion would occur about one million times slower<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p1225_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p1225-184">[182]</a></sup> at the normal blood <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of 7 or would require a pH of 10 or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotton1999p627_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton1999p627-185">[183]</a></sup> The non-related β-carbonic anhydrase is required in plants for leaf formation, the synthesis of indole <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a> (auxin) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic_fermentation" title="Alcoholic fermentation">alcoholic fermentation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carboxypeptidase cleaves peptide linkages during digestion of proteins. A <a href="/wiki/Coordinate_covalent_bond" title="Coordinate covalent bond">coordinate covalent bond</a> is formed between the terminal peptide and a C=O group attached to zinc, which gives the carbon a positive charge. This helps to create a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobic" title="Hydrophobic">hydrophobic</a> pocket on the enzyme near the zinc, which attracts the non-polar part of the protein being digested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p1224—1225_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p1224—1225-182">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Signalling">Signalling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Signalling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Zinc has been recognized as a messenger, able to activate signalling pathways. Many of these pathways provide the driving force in aberrant cancer growth. They can be targeted through <a href="/wiki/Zinc_transporter_protein" title="Zinc transporter protein">ZIP transporters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_proteins">Other proteins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Other proteins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Zinc serves a purely structural role in <a href="/wiki/Zinc_finger" title="Zinc finger">zinc fingers</a>, twists and clusters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotton1997p628_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton1997p628-188">[186]</a></sup> Zinc fingers form parts of some <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factors</a>, which are proteins that recognize <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DNA_sequence" title="DNA sequence">DNA base sequences</a> during the replication and transcription of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>. Each of the nine or ten <span class="chemf nowrap">Zn<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ions in a zinc finger helps maintain the finger's structure by coordinately binding to four <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> in the transcription factor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p1225_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p1225-184">[182]</a></sup> The transcription factor wraps around the DNA helix and uses its fingers to accurately bind to the DNA sequence.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">blood plasma</a>, zinc is bound to and transported by <a href="/wiki/Albumin" title="Albumin">albumin</a> (60%, low-affinity) and <a href="/wiki/Transferrin" title="Transferrin">transferrin</a> (10%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rink2000_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rink2000-173">[171]</a></sup> Because transferrin also transports iron, excessive iron reduces zinc absorption, and vice versa. A similar antagonism exists with copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitney2005_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitney2005-189">[187]</a></sup> The concentration of zinc in blood plasma stays relatively constant regardless of zinc intake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC2000p447_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-NRC2000p447-190">[188]</a></sup> Cells in the salivary gland, prostate, immune system, and intestine use <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">zinc signaling</a> to communicate with other cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zinc may be held in <a href="/wiki/Metallothionein" title="Metallothionein">metallothionein</a> reserves within microorganisms or in the intestines or liver of animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotton1999p629_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton1999p629-192">[190]</a></sup> Metallothionein in intestinal cells is capable of adjusting absorption of zinc by 15–40%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blake2007_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blake2007-193">[191]</a></sup> However, inadequate or excessive zinc intake can be harmful; excess zinc particularly impairs copper absorption because metallothionein absorbs both metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fosmire1990_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fosmire1990-194">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>The human <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_transporter" title="Dopamine transporter">dopamine transporter</a> contains a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Affinity_(pharmacology)" title="Affinity (pharmacology)">high affinity</a> extracellular zinc <a href="/wiki/Binding_site" title="Binding site">binding site</a> which, upon zinc binding, inhibits dopamine <a href="/wiki/Reuptake" title="Reuptake">reuptake</a> and amplifies <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamine</a>-induced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter_efflux" title="Neurotransmitter efflux">dopamine efflux</a> <i><a href="/wiki/In_vitro" title="In vitro">in vitro</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_binding_sites_+_ADHD_review_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_binding_sites_+_ADHD_review-195">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Review_-_cites_2002_amph-zinc_primary_study_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Review_-_cites_2002_amph-zinc_primary_study-196">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Primary_2002_amph-zinc_study_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Primary_2002_amph-zinc_study-197">[195]</a></sup> The human <a href="/wiki/Serotonin_transporter" title="Serotonin transporter">serotonin transporter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine_transporter" title="Norepinephrine transporter">norepinephrine transporter</a> do not contain zinc binding sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Primary_2002_amph-zinc_study_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-Primary_2002_amph-zinc_study-197">[195]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_recommendations">Dietary recommendations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Dietary recommendations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The U.S. Institute of Medicine (IOM) updated Estimated Average Requirements (EARs) and Recommended Dietary Allowances (RDAs) for zinc in 2001. The current EARs for zinc for women and men ages 14 and up is 6.8 and 9.4 mg/day, respectively. The RDAs are 8 and 11 mg/day. RDAs are higher than EARs so as to identify amounts that will cover people with higher than average requirements. RDA for pregnancy is 11 mg/day. RDA for lactation is 12 mg/day. For infants up to 12 months the RDA is 3 mg/day. For children ages 1–13 years the RDA increases with age from 3 to 8 mg/day. As for safety, the IOM sets <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tolerable_upper_intake_level" title="Tolerable upper intake level">Tolerable upper intake levels</a> (ULs) for vitamins and minerals when evidence is sufficient. In the case of zinc the adult UL is 40 mg/day (lower for children). Collectively the EARs, RDAs, AIs and ULs are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Dietary Reference Intakes</a> (DRIs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[196]</a></sup>
