</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority" title="European Food Safety Authority">European Food Safety Authority</a> (EFSA) refers to the collective set of information as Dietary Reference Values, with Population Reference Intake (PRI) instead of RDA, and Average Requirement instead of EAR. AI and UL are defined the same as in United States. For people ages 18 and older the PRI calculations are complex, as the EFSA has set higher and higher values as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phytate" title="Phytate">phytate</a> content of the diet increases. For women, PRIs increase from 7.5 to 12.7 mg/day as phytate intake increases from 300 to 1200 mg/day; for men the range is 9.4 to 16.3 mg/day. These PRIs are higher than the U.S. RDAs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[197]</a></sup> The EFSA reviewed the same safety question and set its UL at 25 mg/day, which is much lower than the U.S. value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>For U.S. food and dietary supplement labeling purposes the amount in a serving is expressed as a percent of Daily Value (%DV). For zinc labeling purposes 100% of the Daily Value was 15 mg, but as of May 27, 2016 it has been revised to 11 mg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FedReg_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FedReg-201">[199]</a></sup> A table of the old and new adult Daily Values is provided at <a href="/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake" title="Reference Daily Intake">Reference Daily Intake</a>. Food and supplement companies have until January 1, 2020 to comply with the change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDAdelay_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FDAdelay-202">[200]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_intake">Dietary intake</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Dietary intake">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Foodstuff-containing-Zinc.jpg"><img alt="Several plates full of various cereals, fruits and vegetables on a table." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="969" data-file-width="640" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Foodstuff-containing-Zinc.jpg/170px-Foodstuff-containing-Zinc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Foodstuff-containing-Zinc.jpg/255px-Foodstuff-containing-Zinc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Foodstuff-containing-Zinc.jpg/340px-Foodstuff-containing-Zinc.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Foodstuff-containing-Zinc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recommended_Dietary_Allowance" title="Recommended Dietary Allowance">Foods and spices</a> containing zinc</div></div></div>
<p>Animal products such as meat, fish, shellfish, fowl, eggs, and dairy contain zinc. The concentration of zinc in plants varies with the level in the soil. With adequate zinc in the soil, the food plants that contain the most zinc are wheat (germ and bran) and various seeds, including <a href="/wiki/Sesame" title="Sesame">sesame</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poppy" title="Poppy">poppy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alfalfa" title="Alfalfa">alfalfa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celery" title="Celery">celery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mustard_(condiment)" title="Mustard (condiment)">mustard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger1993_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger1993-203">[201]</a></sup> Zinc is also found in <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">beans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nut_(fruit)" title="Nut (fruit)">nuts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Almond" title="Almond">almonds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whole_grain" title="Whole grain">whole grains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pumpkin_seed" title="Pumpkin seed">pumpkin seeds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sunflower_seed" title="Sunflower seed">sunflower seeds</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blackcurrant" title="Blackcurrant">blackcurrant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDA_Zn_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-USDA_Zn-204">[202]</a></sup> Plant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phytate" title="Phytate">phytates</a> are particularly found in pulses and cereals and interfere with zinc absorption.
</p><p>Other sources include <a href="/wiki/Food_fortification" title="Food fortification">fortified food</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">dietary supplements</a> in various forms. A 1998 review concluded that zinc oxide, one of the most common supplements in the United States, and zinc carbonate are nearly insoluble and poorly absorbed in the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen1998_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen1998-205">[203]</a></sup> This review cited studies that found lower plasma zinc concentrations in the subjects who consumed zinc oxide and zinc carbonate than in those who took zinc acetate and sulfate salts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen1998_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allen1998-205">[203]</a></sup> For fortification, however, a 2003 review recommended cereals (containing zinc oxide) as a cheap, stable source that is as easily absorbed as the more expensive forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[204]</a></sup> A 2005 study found that various compounds of zinc, including oxide and sulfate, did not show statistically significant differences in absorption when added as fortificants to maize tortillas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deficiency">Deficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Deficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Zinc_deficiency" title="Zinc deficiency">Zinc deficiency</a></div>
<p>Zinc deficiency is usually due to insufficient dietary intake, but can be associated with <a href="/wiki/Malabsorption" title="Malabsorption">malabsorption</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acrodermatitis_enteropathica" title="Acrodermatitis enteropathica">acrodermatitis enteropathica</a>, chronic liver disease, chronic renal disease, sickle cell disease, diabetes, malignancy, and other chronic illnesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prasad2003_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Prasad2003-10">[10]</a></sup> Groups at risk of zinc deficiency include the elderly, children in developing countries, and those with renal dysfunction.
</p><p>In the United States, a federal survey of food consumption determined that for women and men over the age of 19, average consumption was 9.7 and 14.2 mg/day, respectively. For women, 17% consumed less than the EAR, for men 11%. The percentages below EAR increased with age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[206]</a></sup> The most recent published update of the survey (NHANES 2013–2014) reported lower averages – 9.3 and 13.2 mg/day – again with intake decreasing with age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[207]</a></sup>
</p><p>Symptoms of mild zinc deficiency are diverse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC2000p442_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-NRC2000p442-210">[208]</a></sup> Clinical outcomes include depressed growth, diarrhea, impotence and delayed sexual maturation, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alopecia" title="Alopecia">alopecia</a>, eye and skin lesions, impaired appetite, altered cognition, impaired host defense properties, defects in carbohydrate utilization, and reproductive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teratogenesis" title="Teratogenesis">teratogenesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC2000p447_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-NRC2000p447-190">[188]</a></sup> Mild zinc deficiency depresses immunity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[209]</a></sup> although excessive zinc does also.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rink2000_173-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rink2000-173">[171]</a></sup> Animals with a zinc deficiency require twice as much food to attain the same weight gain as animals with sufficient zinc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCp4-42_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-CRCp4-42-132">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite some concerns,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada_2003_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canada_2003-212">[210]</a></sup> western vegetarians and vegans do not suffer any more from overt zinc deficiency than meat-eaters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[211]</a></sup> Major plant sources of zinc include cooked dried beans, sea vegetables, fortified cereals, soy foods, nuts, peas, and seeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada_2003_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-Canada_2003-212">[210]</a></sup> However, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phytates" title="Phytates">phytates</a> in many whole-grains and fibers may interfere with zinc absorption and marginal zinc intake has poorly understood effects. The zinc <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelator</a> <a href="/wiki/Phytic_acid" title="Phytic acid">phytate</a>, found in seeds and <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereal</a> <a href="/wiki/Bran" title="Bran">bran</a>, can contribute to zinc malabsorption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prasad2003_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Prasad2003-10">[10]</a></sup> Some evidence suggests that more than the US RDA (15 mg) of zinc daily may be needed in those whose diet is high in phytates, such as some vegetarians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada_2003_212-2"><a href="#cite_note-Canada_2003-212">[210]</a></sup> These considerations must be balanced against the paucity of adequate zinc <a href="/wiki/Biomarker" title="Biomarker">biomarkers</a>, and the most widely used indicator, plasma zinc, has poor <a href="/wiki/Sensitivity_and_specificity" title="Sensitivity and specificity">sensitivity and specificity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[212]</a></sup> Diagnosing zinc deficiency is a persistent challenge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hambridge2007_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hambridge2007-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nearly two billion people in the developing world are deficient in zinc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prasad2003_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Prasad2003-10">[10]</a></sup> In children, it causes an increase in infection and diarrhea and contributes to the death of about 800,000 children worldwide per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hambridge2007_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hambridge2007-9">[9]</a></sup> The World Health Organization advocates zinc supplementation for severe malnutrition and diarrhea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2007_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2007-215">[213]</a></sup> Zinc supplements help prevent disease and reduce mortality, especially among children with low birth weight or stunted growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2007_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2007-215">[213]</a></sup> However, zinc supplements should not be administered alone, because many in the developing world have several deficiencies, and zinc interacts with other micronutrients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[214]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soil_remediation">Soil remediation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Soil remediation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Species of <i><a href="/wiki/Calluna" title="Calluna">Calluna</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Erica" title="Erica">Erica</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Vaccinium" title="Vaccinium">Vaccinium</a></i> can grow in zinc metalliferous soils, because translocation of toxic ions is prevented by the action of <a href="/wiki/Ericoid_mycorrhiza" title="Ericoid mycorrhiza">ericoid mycorrhizal fungi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[215]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Zinc deficiency appears to be the most common micronutrient deficiency in crop plants; it is particularly common in high-pH soils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[216]</a></sup>  Zinc-deficient <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> is <a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">cultivated</a> in the cropland of about half of Turkey and India, a third of China, and most of Western Australia.  Substantial responses to zinc fertilization have been reported in these areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broadley2007_99-4"><a href="#cite_note-Broadley2007-99">[98]</a></sup> Plants that grow in soils that are zinc-deficient are more susceptible to disease. Zinc is added to the soil primarily through the weathering of rocks, but humans have added zinc through fossil fuel combustion, mine waste, phosphate fertilizers, pesticide (<a href="/wiki/Zinc_phosphide" title="Zinc phosphide">zinc phosphide</a>), limestone, manure, sewage sludge, and particles from galvanized surfaces. Excess zinc is toxic to plants, although zinc toxicity is far less widespread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broadley2007_99-5"><a href="#cite_note-Broadley2007-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Zinc_toxicity" title="Zinc toxicity">Zinc toxicity</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Toxicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although zinc is an essential requirement for good health, excess zinc can be harmful. Excessive absorption of zinc suppresses copper and iron absorption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fosmire1990_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fosmire1990-194">[192]</a></sup> The free zinc ion in solution is highly toxic to plants, invertebrates, and even vertebrate fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[217]</a></sup> The Free Ion Activity Model is well-established in the literature, and shows that just <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">micromolar</a> amounts of the free ion kills some organisms. A recent example showed 6 micromolar killing 93% of all <i><a href="/wiki/Daphnia" title="Daphnia">Daphnia</a></i> in water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p>The free zinc ion is a powerful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acid</a> up to the point of being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corrosive" title="Corrosive">corrosive</a>. Stomach acid contains <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>, in which metallic zinc dissolves readily to give corrosive zinc chloride. Swallowing a post-1982 American one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cent_(United_States_coin)" title="Cent (United States coin)">cent</a> piece (97.5% zinc) can cause damage to the stomach lining through the high solubility of the zinc ion in the acidic stomach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>Evidence shows that people taking 100–300 mg of zinc daily may suffer induced <a href="/wiki/Copper_deficiency" title="Copper deficiency">copper deficiency</a>. A 2007 trial observed that elderly men taking 80 mg daily were hospitalized for urinary complications more often than those taking a placebo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[220]</a></sup> Levels of 100–300 mg may interfere with the utilization of copper and iron or adversely affect cholesterol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fosmire1990_194-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fosmire1990-194">[192]</a></sup> Zinc in excess of 500 ppm in soil interferes with the plant absorption of other essential metals, such as iron and manganese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p504_100-3"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p504-100">[99]</a></sup> A condition called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_shakes" title="Zinc shakes">zinc shakes</a> or "zinc chills" can be induced by inhalation of zinc fumes while <a href="/wiki/Brazing" title="Brazing">brazing</a> or welding galvanized materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCp4-42_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-CRCp4-42-132">[130]</a></sup> Zinc is a common ingredient of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denture" title="Denture">denture</a> cream which may contain between 17 and 38 mg of zinc per gram. Disability and even deaths from excessive use of these products have been claimed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[221]</a></sup>
</p><p>The U.S. <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA) states that zinc damages nerve receptors in the nose, causing <a href="/wiki/Anosmia" title="Anosmia">anosmia</a>. Reports of anosmia were also observed in the 1930s when zinc preparations were used in a failed attempt to prevent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polio" title="Polio">polio</a> infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[222]</a></sup> On June 16, 2009, the FDA ordered removal of zinc-based intranasal cold products from store shelves. The FDA said the loss of smell can be life-threatening because people with impaired smell cannot detect leaking gas or smoke, and cannot tell if food has spoiled before they eat it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAT_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-LAT-225">[223]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent research suggests that the topical antimicrobial zinc pyrithione is a potent <a href="/wiki/Heat_shock" title="Heat shock">heat shock</a> response inducer that may impair genomic integrity with induction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poly_ADP_ribose_polymerase" title="Poly ADP ribose polymerase">PARP</a>-dependent energy crisis in cultured human <a href="/wiki/Keratinocyte" title="Keratinocyte">keratinocytes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Melanocyte" title="Melanocyte">melanocytes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[224]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poisoning">Poisoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Poisoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1982, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Mint" title="United States Mint">US Mint</a> began minting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cent_(United_States_coin)" title="Cent (United States coin)">pennies</a> coated in copper but containing primarily zinc. Zinc pennies pose a risk of zinc toxicosis, which can be fatal. One reported case of chronic ingestion of 425 pennies (over 1 kg of zinc) resulted in death due to gastrointestinal bacterial and fungal <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a>. Another patient who ingested 12 grams of zinc showed only <a href="/wiki/Lethargy" title="Lethargy">lethargy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ataxia" title="Ataxia">ataxia</a> (gross lack of coordination of muscle movements).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[225]</a></sup> Several other cases have been reported of humans suffering zinc intoxication by the ingestion of zinc coins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[227]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pennies and other small coins are sometimes ingested by dogs, requiring veterinary removal of the foreign objects. The zinc content of some coins can cause zinc toxicity, commonly fatal in dogs through severe <a href="/wiki/Hemolytic_anemia" title="Hemolytic anemia">hemolytic anemia</a> and liver or kidney damage; vomiting and diarrhea are possible symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[228]</a></sup> Zinc is highly toxic in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parrots" title="Parrots">parrots</a> and poisoning can often be fatal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[229]</a></sup> The consumption of fruit juices stored in galvanized cans has resulted in mass parrot poisonings with zinc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p501_57-5"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p501-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="mw-references-wrap"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zinc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">

<li id="cite_note-147"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-147">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Kim, Jeung Gon; Walsh, Patrick J. (2006). "From Aryl Bromides to Enantioenriched Benzylic Alcohols in a Single Flask: Catalytic Asymmetric Arylation of Aldehydes". <i>Angewandte Chemie International Edition</i>. <b>45</b> (25): 4175–4178. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1002%2Fanie.200600741" rel="nofollow">10.1002/anie.200600741</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16721894" rel="nofollow">16721894</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Angewandte+Chemie+International+Edition&amp;rft.atitle=From+Aryl+Bromides+to+Enantioenriched+Benzylic+Alcohols+in+a+Single+Flask%3A+Catalytic+Asymmetric+Arylation+of+Aldehydes&amp;rft.volume=45&amp;rft.issue=25&amp;rft.pages=4175-4178&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1002%2Fanie.200600741&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F16721894&amp;rft.aulast=Kim&amp;rft.aufirst=Jeung+Gon&amp;rft.au=Walsh%2C+Patrick+J.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AZinc"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-148"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-148">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One-pot_reaction" title="One-pot reaction">one-pot reaction</a> <a href="/wiki/Bromobenzene" title="Bromobenzene">bromobenzene</a> is converted to <a href="/wiki/Phenyllithium" title="Phenyllithium">phenyllithium</a> by reaction with 4 equivalents of <a href="/wiki/N-Butyllithium" title="N-Butyllithium"><i>n</i>-butyllithium</a>, then transmetalation with <a href="/wiki/Zinc_chloride" title="Zinc chloride">zinc chloride</a> forms diphenylzinc that continues to react in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asymmetric_reaction" title="Asymmetric reaction">asymmetric reaction</a> first with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIB_ligand" title="MIB ligand">MIB ligand</a> and then with 2-naphthylaldehyde to the <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a>. In this reaction formation of diphenylzinc is accompanied by that of <a href="/wiki/Lithium_chloride" title="Lithium chloride">lithium chloride</a>, which if unchecked, catalyses the reaction without MIB involvement to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racemic" title="Racemic">racemic</a> <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a>. The salt is effectively removed by <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelation</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetraethylethylene_diamine" title="Tetraethylethylene diamine">tetraethylethylene diamine</a> (TEEDA) resulting in an <a href="/wiki/Enantiomeric_excess" title="Enantiomeric excess">enantiomeric excess</a> of 92%.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review-167"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review_167-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review_167-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review_167-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review_167-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Zinc_&amp;_sleep_2017_review_167-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Cherasse Y, Urade Y (November 2017). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5713303" rel="nofollow">"Dietary Zinc Acts as a Sleep Modulator"</a>. <i>International Journal of Molecular Sciences</i>. <b>18</b> (11): 2334. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.3390%2Fijms18112334" rel="nofollow">10.3390/ijms18112334</a>. <a href="/wiki/PubMed_Central" title="PubMed Central">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5713303" rel="nofollow">5713303</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/29113075" rel="nofollow">29113075</a>. <q>Zinc is the second most abundant trace metal in the human body, and is essential for many biological processes.  ... The trace metal zinc is an essential cofactor for more than 300 enzymes and 1000 transcription factors [16]. ... In the central nervous system, zinc is the second most abundant trace metal and is involved in many processes. In addition to its role in enzymatic activity, it also plays a major role in cell signaling and modulation of neuronal activity.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=International+Journal+of+Molecular+Sciences&amp;rft.atitle=Dietary+Zinc+Acts+as+a+Sleep+Modulator&amp;rft.volume=18&amp;rft.issue=11&amp;rft.pages=2334&amp;rft.date=2017-11&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC5713303&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F29113075&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3390%2Fijms18112334&amp;rft.aulast=Cherasse&amp;rft.aufirst=Y&amp;rft.au=Urade%2C+Y&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC5713303&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AZinc"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>

<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.periodicvideos.com/videos/030.htm" rel="nofollow">Zinc</a> at <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Periodic_Table_of_Videos" title="The Periodic Table of Videos">The Periodic Table of Videos</a></i> (University of Nottingham)</li></ul>
